From hungry dogs when a particular bell rings upon their ears, and he repeated that over consecutive days. Their reaction to the sight of food would appear each time with saliva flowing from their mouths, then he rang the bell alone after that without presenting food to them, and the same effect appeared in them that used to appear at the sight of food. And the explanation of that with respect to what we are discussing: that when the dogs saw the conjunction of the sound of the bell with the appearance of food before them, and this conjunction continued before them for a period of time, this association became established in their perception and influenced them with a certain influence in their souls. And if we said that dogs have a mind proportionate to them with which they think, we would say that they thought, from the long continuation of this conjunction, that the bell is the effective cause in the appearance and presence of food. And the story of man before this existence is nothing but the story of these dogs before the bell and food. For the will of Allah the Most High has been attached to the effect that grass of the earth does not appear except after the descent of rains from the clouds. And His will has been attached to the effect that raindrops do not descend except after the clouds become dense and thicken to a certain extent within a certain degree of humidity, and His will has been attached to the effect that people do not procreate except when they mate… and so on. And if Allah willed, He would separate these matters and cut the connection between them and leave each one of them to go its own way and appear independently of the other. Therefore, what we call the law of causality in the universe is, in reality, nothing but the law of mere conjunction. We have called it thus because it appeared to us in the guise of causality and became established thus in our imaginations."
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage uses an analogy to explain how humans come to believe in necessary causality. It begins by describing Pavlov's experiment: dogs salivate when food appears after a bell; after repeated conjunction, the bell alone produces salivation. The explanation is that the dogs perceive constant conjunction and then imagine the bell to be the cause. Buti then applies this to human experience: just as the dogs form a link between bell and food, humans observe that rain follows clouds, grass appears after rain, children are born after mating, etc. These are all constant conjunctions established by God's will. If God willed, He could break these regularities. The conclusion is that what we call 'causality' is actually only a law of constant conjunction (قانون المقارنة المجردة). Our minds have become accustomed to seeing one after the other and we mistakenly think it is necessary causation. The passage is a definition and proof: defining the nature of perceived causality as conjunction, and proving it through the analogy.

مقارنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Buti denies all causal relationships in the world.
Correction: Buti does not deny that events are regularly connected; he only denies that the connection is inherently necessary. The regularities are established by Allah's will and could be otherwise.

Al-Ghazali's Prior Articulation of Constant Conjunction
Buti notes al-Ghazali's prior articulation of constant conjunction theory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that al-Ghazali had already clearly articulated the theory of constant conjunction in his works, centuries before Pavlov, and that this idea was accessible to discerning scholars.

Core Idea
Constant conjunction theory was not new — al-Ghazali explained it centuries before Pavlov.

In his discussion of causality, Buti makes an important historical note: what is often credited to Pavlov was already laid out by al-Ghazali in his masterworks. This passage serves as both a corrective and a reminder that Islamic scholarship anticipated many modern theories. Let us examine Buti's claim and its significance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation to understand Buti’s point clearly.
Notice the reference to Pavlov—Buti is responding to modern claims.
2
Identify al-Ghazali's works
The passage mentions two of al-Ghazali’s works: al-Mustasfa and Tahafut al-Falasifa. Look up the references if possible to see the theory in context.
These are key texts in Islamic theology and philosophy.
3
Reflect on the implication
Buti says the theory is expressed in 'the simplest humble expression'. Consider what this says about the accessibility of such knowledge to a discerning scholar.
This challenges the idea that constant conjunction is a modern discovery.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في الإشادة ببافلوف وأنه أول مكتشف لهذه النظرية مع أن النظرية نفسها موجودة بأجلى ما يمكن من بيان في كتاب المستصفى وتهافت الفلاسفة للغزالي رحمه الله ، وبأبسط عبارة متواضعة تدل على أن النظرية ليست مما يعجز العالم المتبصر عن معرفته ( راجع المستصفى : ١ / ٥٧ ، وتهافت الفلاسفة : ٢٣٥ )
In praise of Pavlov and that he is the first discoverer of this theory, while the theory itself is present in the clearest possible exposition in the book al-Mustasfa and Tahafut al-Falasifa by al-Ghazali, may Allah have mercy on him, and in the simplest humble expression indicating that the theory is not something that a discerning scholar is incapable of knowing (refer to al-Mustasfa: 1/57, and Tahafut al-Falasifa: 235).

Commentary
This short passage is a critical note. It points out that despite the praise given to Pavlov as the first discoverer of the theory of conditioned reflex (constant conjunction), the same theory is already clearly articulated in al-Ghazali's works, specifically al-Mustasfa and Tahafut al-Falasifa. The author adds that al-Ghazali's expression is simple and accessible, indicating that this theory is not a great discovery but rather something a discerning scholar could understand. This serves as a historical corrective and reinforces that the idea of constant conjunction is not novel but rooted in Islamic thought.

Key Terms
النظرية
المستصفى
تهافت الفلاسفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Buti is dismissing Pavlov entirely and claiming Islamic superiority.
Correction: Buti is not dismissing Pavlov; he is correcting the historical record by noting that al-Ghazali had already clearly expressed the same theory. The point is that the idea of constant conjunction was already known in Islamic thought, not that Pavlov's work has no value.

Checkpoint
Why does Buti mention al-Ghazali's works in this passage?

B) To show that the theory of constant conjunction was already articulated by al-Ghazali.
C) To criticize al-Ghazali for not conducting experiments.
D) To recommend reading al-Ghazali for scientific methodology.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ghazali had already articulated the theory of constant conjunction in his works, centuries before Pavlov.

Causes Are 'Ja'aliyya' (Appointed) Not Inherently Effective
Buti defines the term 'al-asbab al-ja'aliyya' and explains its rationale.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between appointed causes (al-asbab al-ja'aliyya) and inherently effective causes, and why Muslim scholars use this term to describe cosmic regularities.

Core Idea
Causes are appointed by God, not inherently effective.

In this passage, Ramadan al-Buti introduces a critical concept from Islamic theology: the nature of causal relationships in the universe. He explains that scholars deliberately avoid the term 'causality' because it suggests an inherent power in created things. Instead, they use the term 'appointed causes' (al-asbab al-ja'aliyya) to emphasize that these are mere conjunctions established by God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the contrasting phrases: 'أسباب ذاتية مؤثرة' vs 'مقارنة ليس إلا'.
2
Identify the key contrast
Buti contrasts the common designation ('هذه التسمية') with the scholarly term 'الأسباب الجعلية'. Identify what he rejects and what he affirms.
The rejection is implicit: the common term implies inherent causality, which is denied.
3
Grasp the theological implication
Understand that this distinction protects God's absolute power and denies any inherent efficacy to created things. The perceived 'causality' is merely God's habit of conjunction.
This is a core Ash'ari position: God is the only true agent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إلا أن هذه التسمية لا تتفق مع حقائق العلم وواقع الأمور ، كما قد رأيت . فلذلك أطلق العلماء على هذه الأسباب الكونية اسم : الأسباب الجعلية . أي أمور جعلها الله بمحض المقارنة أسباباً ، فهي مجعولة جعلاً وليست أسبـابـاً ذاتية مؤثرة ، وما قد تلمحه فيها من مظاهر التأثير والعلّية ليس كذلك في الحقيقة بل هي المقارنة ليس إلا .
Except that this designation does not agree with the realities of knowledge and the actual state of things, as you have seen. Therefore, the scholars gave to these cosmic causes the name: al-asbab al-ja'aliyya (the appointed causes). That is, matters which God made causes by mere conjunction, so they are causes by appointment (ja'lan) and not inherently effective causes, and what you may perceive in them of manifestations of influence and causality is not so in reality, but rather it is mere conjunction and nothing else.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage defines a key term: الأسباب الجعلية (appointed causes). The author argues that calling these regularities 'causality' (السببية) is inaccurate because it implies inherent influence. Instead, scholars classify them as 'causes by appointment' (جعلية). They are not causes that produce effects intrinsically; rather, God simply established a pattern of conjunction. The apparent efficacy is only in our perception; the reality is constant conjunction. This is a classification: distinguishing between true necessary causation (nonexistent in created things) and the appointed relationship that we observe.

Key Terms
الأسباب الجعلية
جعلية
مقارنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think 'ja'aliyya' denies any real connection between cause and effect.
Correction: It affirms the connection as a divinely ordained conjunction, not random but consistent, yet without inherent power in the cause.

Checkpoint

A) Causes that have inherent power to produce effects.
B) Causes that are merely appointed by God through constant conjunction.
C) Causes that are imaginary and have no reality.
D) Causes that work by necessity.

Key Takeaway
Appointed causes (al-asbab al-ja'aliyya) are mere conjunctions created by God, not independent agents of causation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Al-Ghazali's View of Deposited Power
Al-Ghazali's position on deposited power in causes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ghazali's position that a deposited influence exists in causes, which is not inherent but granted by Allah and removable at His will.

Core Idea
Causes contain a God-deposited power, not automatic effect.

Buti presents al-Ghazali's nuanced stance on causality, which reconciles the Ash'ari principle of ja'aliyya with a real yet contingent influence in causes. In this passage, al-Ghazali asserts that there is a latent power placed by Allah, which He can remove, thus avoiding both necessitarianism and pure occasionalism. This sets the stage for a resolution of the debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify al-Ghazali’s main claim
Read the passage and note that al-Ghazali sees no contradiction between ja'aliyya and deposited influence.
Ja'aliyya means causes are mere conjunctions established by God.
2
Understand the distinction
Notice the contrast between mere conjunction (like bell and food) and latent deposited influence.
The influence is not inherent to the cause.
3
Grasp the condition of removability
Al-Ghazali says God can remove this deposited power at will, nullifying the cause's causality.
This preserves divine omnipotence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
غير أن الإمام الغزالي رحمه الله لا يرى تنافياً بين أن تكون الأسباب الكونية جعلية كما قلنا ، وبين أن يكون فيها تأثير أودعه الله عز وجل فيها يسلبه عنها عندما يشاء . وهو يرى أن هذا هو الحق . أي فالمسألة ليست مسألة مقارنة مجردة ، كمقارنة الجرس للطعام ، بل هنالك تأثير كامن في السبب المقارن ، ولكنه ليس تأثيراً منبثقاً من ذاته بل مودع فيه من قبل الله عز وجل . فإذا أراد تعطيل السبب عن سببيته أزال عنه هذه القوة المودعة فيه . وتحليل الغزالي هذا ، أقرب إلى الانسجام مع التعليلات العلمية لظواهر الأشياء وتكويناتها ، إلا أنه أبعد عن مسلك الجمهور وما اتفق عليه . ونحن نرى أن الخلاف ينتهي بعد مراحل يسيرة من النظر ، إلى الوفاق . فهو خلاف لفظي إذاً . إذ المقصود أن تأثير الأسباب الكونية ليس تأثيراً حتمياً وإنما هو بإرادة الله عز وجل فهي أمور لا علاقة لها في الأصل بغيرها . ولكن الله جعلها أسباباً لها سواء قلنا إنه أودع فيها قوة مؤثرة . أو لم يودع فيها هذه القوة
But Imam al-Ghazali, may Allah have mercy on him, does not see a contradiction between the cosmic causes being 'ja'aliyya' as we said, and between there being in them an influence that Allah the Mighty and Majestic has deposited in them, which He strips from them when He wills. And he sees that this is the truth. That is, the matter is not a matter of mere conjunction, like the conjunction of the bell with food, but rather there is a latent influence in the conjoined cause, but it is not an influence emanating from itself, but rather deposited in it by Allah the Mighty and Majestic. So if He wants to nullify the cause from its causality, He removes this deposited power from it. And al-Ghazali's analysis is closer to harmony with scientific explanations of the phenomena and formations of things, but it is farther from the path of the majority and what they agreed upon. And we see that the disagreement ends, after a few stages of reflection, in agreement. So it is a verbal disagreement, then. For the intended meaning is that the influence of the cosmic causes is not a necessary influence, but rather it is by the will of Allah the Mighty and Majestic, so they are matters that originally have no relation to other than themselves. But God made them causes for them, whether we say He deposited in them an effective power or He did not deposit this power.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage presents al-Ghazali's nuanced position and resolves the apparent conflict. It begins by stating al-Ghazali's view: that the Ash'ari notion of 'ja'aliyya' (causes as mere conjunction) does not necessarily preclude a power deposited by God in the cause, which He can remove at will. For al-Ghazali, a latent influence exists, but it is not inherent—it is granted by God. This view, the author says, is more aligned with scientific explanations. However, it departs from the majority of Ash'aris (the 'path of the majority'). The author then resolves the disagreement: after reflection, it becomes clear that the difference is merely verbal. Both sides agree that the influence of causes is not necessary but dependent on God's will; the only question is whether to call that influence a 'deposited power' or not. The substance is the same. This is an objection-and-answer: the objection would be that the two positions are contradictory, and the answer is that they are not; the conflict is verbal, not substantive.

Key Terms
تأثير مودع
سببية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think al-Ghazali denied any real effect in causes, like the majority Ash'aris.
Correction: Al-Ghazali actually affirmed a deposited power, though not inherent, thus maintaining a real effect contingent on God's will.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ghazali as presented here, what is the nature of the influence in causes?
A) It is inherent and necessary.
B) It is deposited by Allah and removable.
C) It is merely a conjunction.
D) It is an illusion.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ghazali affirms a deposited power in causes that is contingent on Allah's will, reconciling causal efficacy with divine sovereignty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you experience a success or a blessing through a means (like medicine, effort, or a person), do you instinctively attribute it to that means as if it had independent power? The passage contrasts mere conjunction (bell with food) with latent deposited power. Think of a recent event where you observed a cause and effect. Did you view it as a mechanical conjunction, or did you sense the hidden hand of Allah sustaining the cause? What would it mean for your faith to see every natural event as a theater of divine power? Commit to a specific prayer or dhikr that reminds you of this reality before you sleep tonight.

The Verbal Dispute: Al-Ghazali and the Majority
Buti resolves the dispute as verbal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Buti resolves the apparent disagreement between al-Ghazali and the majority Ash'aris as a merely verbal dispute.

Core Idea
The disagreement over causality is verbal, not substantive.

After presenting al-Ghazali's view, Buti shows that upon reflection, the conflict with the majority disappears. Both sides agree that causes are not inherently effective but depend on God's will. The only difference is whether to label that effectiveness as a 'deposited power' or not. This lesson examines that resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the resolution passage
Pay attention to Buti's analysis and his conclusion that the disagreement is verbal.
2
Identify the common ground
Both sides agree that causality is not necessary but by God's will.
The core meaning is identical.
3
Understand 'verbal disagreement'
The difference is only in wording: deposited power or not, but substance is same.
This resolves the apparent conflict.

The Wisdom Behind the Law of Causality
Buti raises a question about the wisdom of causality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the significance of Buti's question about the wisdom behind the law of causality and the implications of the term 'ja'aliyya' for the contingency of natural laws.

Core Idea
The question 'why' reveals that causality is not self-explanatory but points to divine wisdom.

This lesson introduces a pivotal question in the discussion of causality. After establishing that causes are appointed by God (ja'aliyya) without inherent power, Buti raises the natural question: why did God choose to create such a regular system? This question opens the door to understanding the divine purpose behind the law of causality, a theme that will unfold in subsequent sections.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the translation. Note that the passage is a section heading followed by a question.
The passage is cut off at the end of the page, so only the question is provided.
2
Identify the key terms and the question
Identify the terms 'الحکمة' (wisdom) and 'جعلية' (appointed). The question asks: if causes are ja'aliyya, why did God make them so? Why not separate them?
The term ja'aliyya implies that causes do not act by themselves but are assigned by God.
3
Reflect on the implication of the question
Think about why this question matters. It challenges the assumption that the regular cause-effect sequence is necessary. Instead, it invites us to consider the wisdom behind God's choice to create a predictable universe.
This connects to the broader theme of divine wisdom (ḥikma) in creation.

C - The wisdom of the universe’s subjection to the law of causality: And the question that must be raised here is: If these causes are 'ja'aliyya' as I said, then why did God make them so? Why did He not separate these conjoined matters and distance them from?

Commentary
This passage introduces a new section (جـ) posing a natural question: if causes are merely appointed by God (ja'aliyya) without inherent power, what is the wisdom (الحكمة) in subjecting the universe to this law? Why does God choose to work through regular patterns rather than directly creating effects without intermediate signs? The text begins the question but is truncated at page end, so the answer is not provided here. The question itself is clear: if God could do everything directly, what purpose does the regular sequence serve? This is an objection-style question that anticipates an explanation of divine wisdom.

The Nature of Empirical Causes: Imposed Causality and the Verbal Dispute
Buti explains the ja'liyya nature of empirical causes and resolves the dispute as verbal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why empirical causes are called 'al-asbab al-ja'liyya' and how the dispute between al-Ghazali and the majority is ultimately verbal.

Core Idea
Empirical causes are not inherently effective; they are divinely imposed accompaniments.

Buti addresses a common misunderstanding about causality. While people often assume causes naturally produce effects, scholars clarify that empirical causes are called 'al-asbab al-ja'liyya' – causes of imposition. They are mere accompaniments instituted by God. Yet, al-Ghazali proposed that God deposits an influence in them, which He can withdraw. In this passage, Buti presents both views and argues that the disagreement is merely verbal, as both affirm divine sovereignty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation slowly. Notice how Buti contrasts the common naming with the scholarly term 'al-asbab al-ja'liyya'.
Pay attention to the phrase 'بمحض المقارنة' (by mere accompaniment).
2
Identify the two positions
Find where Buti presents the majority view (mere accompaniment) and where he presents al-Ghazali's view (deposited power).
Ghazali's view includes 'تأثير مودع' (deposited influence).
3
Understand why it's a verbal dispute
Buti says the dispute ends in agreement. Reflect on the common ground: both sides deny inherent, necessary causality and affirm that all effect is by God's will.
The difference is only whether to posit a deposited power or not.
4
Consider the implications
Think about how this understanding affects the way we view natural phenomena and divine intervention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إلا أن هذه التسمية لا تتفق مع حقائق العلم وواقع الأمور ، كما قد رأيت . فلذلك أطلق العلماء على هذه الأسباب الكونية اسم : الأسباب الجعلية . أي أمور جعلها الله بمحض المقارنة أسباباً ، فهي مجعولة جعلاً وليست أسبـابـاً ذاتية مؤثرة ، وما قد تلمحه فيها من مظاهر التأثير والعلّية ليس كذلك في الحقيقة بل هي المقارنة ليس إلا . غير أن الإمام الغزالي رحمه الله لا يرى تنافياً بين أن تكون الأسباب الكونية جعلية كما قلنا ، وبين أن يكون فيها تأثير أودعه الله عز وجل فيها يسلبه عنها عندما يشاء . وهو يرى أن هذا هو الحق . أي فالمسألة ليست مسألة مقارنة مجردة ، كمقارنة الجرس للطعام ، بل هنالك تأثير كامن في السبب المقارن ، ولكنه ليس تأثيراً منبثقاً من ذاته بل مودع فيه من قبل الله عز وجل . فإذا أراد تعطيل السبب عن سببيته أزال عنه هذه القوة المودعة فيه (۱) وتحليل الغزالي هذا ، أقرب إلى الانسجام مع التعليلات العلمية لظواهر الأشياء وتكويناتها ، إلا أنه أبعد عن مسلك الجمهور وما اتفق عليه . ونحن نرى أن الخلاف ينتهي بعد مراحل يسيرة من النظر ، إلى الوفاق . فهو خلاف لفظي إذاً . إذ المقصود أن تأثير الأسباب الكونية ليس تأثيراً حتمياً وإنما هو بإرادة الله عز وجل فهي أمور لا علاقة لها في الأصل بغيرها . ولكن الله جعلها أسباباً لها سواء قلنا إنه أودع فيها قوة مؤثرة . أو لم يودع فيها هذه القوة
However, this naming does not agree with the realities of knowledge and the actual state of affairs, as you have seen. Therefore, scholars have called these cosmic causes by the name: al-asbab al-ja'liyya (causes of imposition). That is, matters that God has made causes by mere accompaniment, so they are made causes by imposition and are not self-subsistent effective causes. And whatever you may perceive in them of manifestations of influence and causality is not truly so, but it is mere accompaniment. However, Imam al-Ghazali (may God have mercy on him) does not see a contradiction between the cosmic causes being ja'liyya as we said, and there being an influence that God Almighty has deposited in them, which He removes from them when He wills. He sees that this is the truth. That is, the matter is not a matter of mere accompaniment, like the accompaniment of a bell to food, but there is a latent influence in the accompanying cause, but it is not an influence emanating from its own essence, rather it is deposited in it by God Almighty. So if He wills to disable the cause from its causality, He removes from it this deposited power. (1) And this analysis of al-Ghazali is more in harmony with scientific explanations of the phenomena and formations of things, except that it is further from the path of the majority and what they agreed upon. We see that the dispute, after a few stages of consideration, ends in agreement. So it is a verbal dispute then. For the intended meaning is that the influence of cosmic causes is not a necessary influence, but is by the will of God Almighty; they are matters that have no connection in origin with other than themselves. But God made them causes for them, whether we say that He has deposited in them an effective force or He has not deposited this force in them.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage explains the nature of empirical causes and resolves a key theological debate. Buti begins by stating that the common people's naming of causes as 'natural' or 'effective' does not match reality. Instead, scholars call them 'al-asbab al-ja'liyya' — causes of divine imposition. These are matters that God has made causes by mere accompaniment, not because they inherently produce effects. Any perceived influence is not actual but only coincidental. Buti then introduces Imam al-Ghazali's view, which does not deny causality outright but holds that God has deposited an influence in the cause, which He can remove at will. Ghazali sees this as the truth, reconciling divine omnipotence with observable regularity. Buti notes that Ghazali's analysis is more compatible with scientific explanations but differs from the majority of scholars. Finally, Buti argues that the dispute is ultimately verbal: both sides agree that causes have no necessary, independent power and that all effect is by God's will. The only difference is whether to posit a deposited power or not, but the underlying belief in divine sovereignty remains the same.

المقارنة
التأثير المودع
الخلاف اللفظي

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that Buti denies all causality or that causes are purely illusory.
Correction: Buti affirms that causes are real as divine institutions, but they are not inherently effective. They are accompaniments, and the influence, if any, is deposited by God.

Checkpoint
What is meant by 'al-asbab al-ja'liyya' (causes of imposition)?
A) Causes that inherently produce their effects by their own essence.
B) Causes that God has made causes by mere accompaniment, without intrinsic effect.
C) Causes that are imaginary and have no relation to phenomena.
D) Causes that are necessary and cannot be altered by God.

Key Takeaway
The dispute over whether causes have deposited power is ultimately verbal, as both sides affirm that all causality is subordinate to God's will.

?

?
You accept that God is the sole creator, but when a plan fails despite all causes being in place, do you react with patience or panic? If you truly internalized that every cause is merely a veil, what would your reaction become?

The Wisdom of Causality in Pointing to God
Buti explains the wisdom behind God's establishment of causes, showing they lead to recognizing the Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the law of causality, far from being a veil, actually reveals the existence of the Creator through the harmony and interdependence of the universe.

Core Idea
Causality is not a curtain; it is the bridge that leads to the First Cause.

Buti anticipates a critical question: If God directly creates causes, why not simply create events without apparent causes? The answer lies in the very harmony that causality produces. This lesson explores how the interconnectedness of causes becomes the most eloquent proof of a single Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Note the question Buti raises and how he answers it.
The text ends mid-sentence; this is not a problem for understanding the argument.
2
Identify the key link: harmony = causality
Buti states that the most prominent indication of the Creator is harmony, and harmony is nothing but the phenomenon of causality. See how this connects.
3
Trace the logical progression
Reflect on how harmony requires ordering, which leads to a regression of causes, ultimately ending in the one great Cause. This is the central argument.
Use the watch analogy to solidify the point.
4
Apply the watch analogy
Imagine scattered parts assembling into a watch. This illustrates how harmony points to a single maker. Connect this to the universe's harmony pointing to God.

جـ - الحكمة من خضوع الكون لقانون السببية : والسؤال الذي لا بد من إيراده هنا هو : فإذا كانت هذه الأسباب جعلية كما قلت ، ففيم جعلها الله كذلك ، وهلا فرق بين هذه الأمور المجتمعة وأبعدها عن بعضها ، حتى لا ينخدع بها الناس فيتوهموها أسبـابـاً مـؤثرة ، وهي ليست كذلك . والجواب : إن أبرز مظاهر دلالة هذا الكون على وجود الخالق عز وجل ، إنما هو مظهر التناسق والانسجام فيه ، كما أوضحنا ذلك من قبل . وليس معنى التناسق والانسجام فيه شيئاً غير ظاهرة السببية والعلية الشائعة والسارية في كل صوره وأجزائه : إذن فلكي يدلَّ الكون دلالة باهرة على وجود الله عز وجل ينبغي أن يكون متناسقاً . ولكي يتم فيه التناسق ، ينبغي أن يكون مرتباً بعضه على بعض بأن يكون هذا محتاجاً وذاك محتاجاً إليه فيتلاقيان طبقاً للحاجة التي بينهما . فإذا تجلى لك من الكون هذا التناسق ، تنبهت لما قلناه من ضرورة تناقص العلل في المسائل المتناسقة كلما أمعنت النظر أكثر ، وكلما سبرت مزيداً من أغوار هذه العلل والمعلولات ؛ فتسير متأملاً في هذا السبيل ، إلى أن تنتهي بك هذه العلل الكثيرة المختلفة إلى العلة الوحيدة الكبرى الكامنة خلف كلّ ما قد رأيت ، أي إلى واجب الوجود وهو الله عز وجل . إنَّ الذي يتأمل أجهزة وآلات معينة ومنتشرة ، لا يمكن أن يصدق أنها جميعاً من صنع شخص واحد هو الموجد لها ، إلا إذا تأمل فرآها متممة بعضها لبعض ، متعاونة لدى التركيب في إيجاد عمل نوعي معين ، وكلما ازداد لمساً لهذا الانسجام وسبر مزيداً من دقائقه ، ازداد يقيناً بوحدة الصانع وذلك كأن يعمد فيركبها إلى بعضها تركيبها الصحيح المتصور وإذا هي قد انقلبت في يده سـاعـة تضبط الزمن ، وإذا هي من صنع معمل معين معروف . وهكذا اقتضت رحمة الله بعباده أن يجعل من كـونـه أفصح بيـان نـاطـق بألوهية الله وحده وبأنه الخالق والمبدع للكون كله ، فجعلك في حاجة مستمرة إلى
C - The wisdom of the universe's submission to the law of causality: And the question that must be raised here is: If these causes are ja'liyya as I said, then why did God make them so? Why did He not separate these assembled matters and distance them from each other, so that people would not be deceived by them and imagine them to be effective causes, while they are not so? The answer: The most prominent manifestation of this universe's indication of the existence of the Creator, Glory be to Him, is the manifestation of harmony and coherence in it, as we have explained before. And the meaning of harmony and coherence in it is nothing other than the phenomenon of causality and causation widespread and flowing in all its forms and parts: So, in order for the universe to indicate in a brilliant way the existence of God Almighty, it must be harmonious. And for harmony to be achieved in it, it must be arranged in succession, such that this is in need and that is needed by it, so they meet according to the need between them. So when this harmony appears to you from the universe, you become aware of what we have said about the necessity of the regression of causes in harmonious matters the more you reflect, and the more you delve into the depths of these causes and effects; you proceed contemplating in this path, until these many different causes lead you to the one great cause latent behind everything you have seen, that is, the Necessary Existence, God Almighty. Indeed, one who contemplates specific and scattered devices and machines cannot believe that they are all the work of a single person who brought them into existence, unless he reflects and sees them completing each other, cooperating in assembly to produce a specific qualitative work, and the more he touches this harmony and delves into its details, the more his certainty in the oneness of the Maker increases. As if he takes them and assembles them together in their correct imagined assembly, and behold, they have turned in his hand into a watch that measures time, and behold, they are from the work of a specific known factory. Thus, the mercy of God towards His servants required that He make of His universe the most eloquent speech declaring the divinity of God alone and that He is the Creator and Originator of the entire universe, so He placed you in continuous need of
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage presents a theological argument for the existence of God based on the regularity of causality. Buti begins by posing a question: If causes are mere impositions by God (ja'liyya), what is the wisdom behind establishing them? Why didn't God just make events occur randomly so that people wouldn't be deceived into thinking causes are effective? The answer is that the universe's most powerful indicator of God's existence is its harmony and coherence. Harmony is nothing but the widespread phenomenon of causality. For the universe to indicate God brilliantly, it must be harmonious. For harmony, parts must be ordered in chains of need and fulfillment. Reflecting on this harmony leads one to see a regression of causes—each cause leads to another, ultimately to the one uncaused cause, the Necessary Existence, God. Buti provides an analogy: a person finding scattered parts cannot believe they form a single product until he sees them fitting together. When assembled correctly, they become a watch from a known maker. Similarly, the intricate interdependence of causes in the universe leads to certainty of the Creator. The passage ends by stating that God's mercy made the universe a clear declaration of His divinity, placing humans in continuous need of... (text cuts off). The argument is essentially a refined cosmological argument from design and dependency.

Correction: The passage argues that the very order and dependence of causes leads to the necessity of a single ultimate Cause, not away from it.

Only Allah Has Real Effect
Buti states the necessary Muslim belief on causality and limits of science.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Muslim belief that only Allah has real causal effect, that all apparent causes are created and contingent, and that science only observes correlation, not necessary connection.

Core Idea
Only Allah has real effect; all causes are created and contingent.

This passage concludes a series of arguments by establishing a core creedal point: nothing in the universe has independent causal power. The author contrasts this with the scientific method, which only observes constant correlation. As a Muslim, you are to affirm absolute divine causality while acknowledging the empirical regularity of causes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's flow come through before you interpret.
Notice the strong language: 'firm belief' and 'no effect from any thing except Allah'.
2
Identify the key distinction
Find where the author distinguishes between real effect (from Allah) and apparent causes (created by Allah). Note the term 'jaliyya' (created).
This is the core of the lesson.
3
Understand the critique of science
The author says science only describes constant correlation, not necessary connection. Reflect on why that supports the belief in Allah's sole causality.
The term 'infisal' (separation) is key.
4
Connect to your own belief
Ask yourself: How does this affect the way you view everyday causes? Do you see them as independent or as veils for Allah's action?

D - What a Muslim must believe based on that. And this last issue is the fruit of all the three issues we have mentioned. A Muslim must hold a firm belief that there is no effect in the universe from any thing except Allah Almighty, and that all that appears to us of manifestations of causes and causal connections are only causes and connections created by Allah Almighty, and that what the researcher may find in them which he calls by science 'factors' and 'effectors' and so on, is only so from the perspective of appearance only. Science has no concern with things except that it describes them as they are in their most precise manifestations, then it practices this description through repeated experimentation. And if science only describes a reality without adding to it, then this reality is nothing more than constant correlation. As for the possibility of separation, that is another matter. And it is far-fetched that science can arrive at the conclusion that the correlation between causes and their effects is necessary, with no alternative to their inseparability and no possibility of their disengagement.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage states the doctrinal conclusion from the preceding discussions. The author identifies it as the fruit of three previous issues. The required belief is firm conviction that no agent has real effect in the universe except Allah. Apparent causes are created by Allah and have no independent causal power. Science only describes constant correlations (what we observe), but cannot prove necessary connection. The possibility of separation (i.e., that the cause could exist without the effect) is not ruled out by science. Thus, the Muslim believes that causality is not intrinsic but contingent upon Allah's will.

Correction: The passage states that science only observes constant correlation, not necessary connection. The possibility of separation (e.g., miracles) remains, affirming Allah's power to intervene.

Key Takeaway

Ruling on Causal Language and Seeking Blessings from Prophets
Buti answers a question on using causal language and extends to seeking blessings from prophets.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand when it is permissible to use causal language about created things and why seeking blessings from prophets is even more permissible.

Core Idea
Causal language is permissible if belief in Allah as the true cause remains intact.

Buti addresses a practical concern: Muslims often use language that attributes effects to created causes. He clarifies that such speech is fine as long as one's belief is sound. He then extends this reasoning to seeking blessings from prophets, showing it is even more acceptable.

Guided Path
1
Identify the question
Read the first part where Buti answers about saying 'this medicine helped me' etc. Note the condition.
2
Understand the condition
The key distinction is whether one believes the created thing causes independently (kufr) or merely appears as a cause while Allah is the true cause (permissible).
3
Apply to prophets
Buti argues that if causal language is permissible for ordinary things, it is even more so for prophets, since they are honored by Allah.
This is an a fortiori argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا ضير في استعمال ألفاظ تدلّ على سببية الأشياء لبعضها إذا سلمت العقيدة :

ولعلك تسأل بعد هذا عن حكم استعمال المسلم ألفاظاً تعبر عن سببية بعض الأشياء وتأثيرها ، وذلك بسبب طول الإلف وظهور هذه الأشياء بمظهر الأسباب الذاتية المؤثرة ، كقول القائل : لقد نفعني هذا الدواء ، وشفاني هذا الطبيب وأينع الزرع بكثرة الأمطار . وكتوسل المسلم بالأنبياء وتبركه بشيء من آثارهم ، وفضلاتهم . فالجواب ، أن ذلك إذا صاحب اعتقاداً بتأثير واحد من هؤلاء ، فهو كفر بالاتفاق كما أوضحنا ذلك آنفاً . أما إن صاحبه الاعتقاد بأن المؤثر في ذلك إنما هو الله جل جلاله ، فلا ضير فيه ، لأن تعبيره هذا جاء موافقاً لظاهر ما أقيم الكون عليه من قانون السببية الجعلية

ولا ضير في التوسل بالرسل والأنبياء من باب أولى :

وإذا كان ذلك بالنسبة للدواء والطبيب والأمطار ، لا ضير فيه ولا حرج ، فلأن لا يكون أي ضير في ذلك بالنسبة للأنبياء كالتوسل والتبرك بهم أولى
There is no harm in using words that indicate the causality of things for each other if the belief is sound.

Perhaps you ask after this about the ruling of a Muslim using words that express the causality and effect of some things, due to long familiarity and the appearance of these things as effective intrinsic causes, like one's saying: 'This medicine has benefited me,' 'This doctor cured me,' and 'The crop has become abundant due to much rain.' And like a Muslim's seeking intercession via the prophets and seeking blessing from something of their effects and remains. The answer: that if it is accompanied by a belief in the effect of any one of these, then it is disbelief by consensus, as we clarified earlier. But if it is accompanied by the belief that the true effecter is Allah, majestic is His glory, then there is no harm in it, because his expression is in accordance with the apparent way the universe has been established upon the law of created causation.

And there is no harm in seeking intercession via the messengers and prophets by even greater reason:

If that is the case concerning medicine, doctor, and rain—there is no harm nor offense in it—then it is even more so that there is no harm in that concerning the prophets, like seeking intercession and seeking blessing from them.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage addresses a practical question: Is it permissible for a Muslim to use language that attributes effects to created things, such as saying 'this medicine helped me' or 'the rain made the crops grow'? The author notes that this might arise from long familiarity. He also addresses seeking blessings from prophets and their relics. The answer is conditional: if such speech is accompanied by the belief that the created thing independently causes the effect, then it is disbelief by consensus. But if the speech is merely an expression of apparent causality while believing that Allah is the true cause, then it is permissible, because it matches the observable law of created causation that Allah has established. Then the author extends this to prophets: if it is permissible for ordinary things like medicine, then it is even more permissible for prophets, since they are honored by Allah. This is an 'a fortiori' argument (من باب أولى).

Key Terms
سببية جعلية
توسل
تبرك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that any speech implying causation is shirk.
Correction: Buti clarifies it is only shirk if the belief is in independent effect; if belief in Allah as true cause, it is permissible.

Key Takeaway
Causal language is permissible as long as one believes Allah is the true cause, and this applies even more to seeking blessings from prophets.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Thank God the medicine worked', do you ever pause to check if your heart genuinely believes Allah is the Healer, or does the phrase mask a reliance on the medicine itself? How can you make your tongue match your conviction?
?
If you were to ask a prophet for intercession, would your heart feel more secure because of the prophet's status or because of Allah's mercy? What would you need to shift in your trust to make it purely for Allah?
?
This lesson teaches that even causal language is permissible with correct belief. But are you willing to examine the beliefs hidden in your own words? Is there a phrase you use that subtly ascribes power to created things without realizing it? Will you honestly search your speech today?

The Permissibility of Causal Language and Tawassul
The author explains the permissibility of causal language and tawassul with conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the conditions under which it is permissible for a Muslim to use causal language and engage in tawassul (seeking intercession) and tabarruk (seeking blessings).

Core Idea
Causal language and tawassul are permissible when one believes that Allah alone is the true effecter.

This passage addresses a common question: Is it permissible to say 'this medicine healed me' or to seek blessings from the relics of prophets? The author explains that such expressions are not only permissible but also commendable, provided the underlying belief is sound. The key distinction is between attributing independent effect to created things and acknowledging them as mere causes placed by Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic source passage once and then its English translation. Pay attention to the flow: the author first states the ruling, then anticipates a question, and provides a detailed answer.
Notice how the author uses the phrase 'لا ضير' (no harm) to frame the discussion.
2
Identify the two conditions
The author presents two contrasting scenarios: attributing independent effect to a cause (which is kufr) versus acknowledging that the true effecter is Allah while using causal language (which is permissible). Find where this distinction is made.
3
Understand the argument from analogy
The author extends the ruling to tawassul with prophets using an a fortiori (awla) argument: if it is permissible for ordinary things like medicine, it is even more fitting for prophets.
Look for the phrase 'من باب أولى' (a fortiori) and the Quranic evidence.
4
Reflect on the Companions' practice
The author cites the practice of the Companions as evidence for the permissibility of tawassul and tabarruk. Consider how this historical practice supports the ruling.
5
Test your understanding with the checkpoint
Answer the multiple-choice question to ensure you can distinguish between the two conditions described in the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا ضير في استعمال ألفاظ تدلّ على سببية الأشياء لبعضها إذا سلمت العقيدة. ولعلك تسأل بعد هذا عن حكم استعمال المسلم ألفاظاً تعبر عن سببية بعض الأشياء وتأثيرها، وذلك بسبب طول الإلف وظهور هذه الأشياء بمظهر الأسباب الذاتية المؤثرة، كقول القائل: لقد نفعني هذا الدواء، وشفاني هذا الطبيب وأينع الزرع بكثرة الأمطار. وكتوسل المسلم بالأنبياء وتبركه بشيء من آثارهم، وفضلاتهم. فالجواب، أن ذلك إذا صاحب اعتقاداً بتأثير واحد من هؤلاء، فهو كفر بالاتفاق كما أوضحنا ذلك آنفاً. أما إن صاحبه الاعتقاد بأن المؤثر في ذلك إنما هو الله جل جلاله، فلا ضير فيه، لأن تعبيره هذا جاء موافقاً لظاهر ما أقيم الكون عليه من قانون السببية الجعلية. ولا ضير في التوسل بالرسل والأنبياء من باب أولى: وإذا كان ذلك بالنسبة للدواء والطبيب والأمطار، لا ضير فيه ولا حرج، فلأن لا يكون أي ضير في ذلك بالنسبة للأنبياء كالتوسل والتبرك بهم أولى وأجدر.. إذ إن الله عز وجل قد جعل نبيه محمداً رحمة للعالمين وأوضح ذلك بصريح كتابه عندما قال: {وما أرسلناك إلا رحمة للعالمين} [الأنبياء 107] فقد جعله الله سبباً لرحمة العباد، وأي ضير في أن يتوسل المسلم بهذا الذي شرفه الله هذا التشريف فجعله رحمة للخلائق؟ وقد امتلأ الصحابة كلهم شعوراً بهذا المعنى، فلم يألوا جهداً في التوسل والتبرك بآثاره وفضلاته في مناسبات كثيرة مختلفة، وقد ثبت ذلك بأحاديث ثابتة صحيحة لا يرقى إليها الظن أو الوهم.
There is no harm in using words that indicate the causality of things for one another when the creed is sound. And perhaps you will ask after this about the ruling on a Muslim using words that express the causality and effect of some things, due to long familiarity and the appearance of these things as effective self-causes, such as one saying: 'This medicine benefited me,' 'This doctor cured me,' and 'The crop flourished due to abundant rain.' And likewise a Muslim's seeking intercession (tawassul) through the prophets and seeking blessings (tabarruk) through some of their relics and remnants. The answer is that if this is accompanied by a belief in the effect of any one of these, then it is kufr by agreement, as we have clarified earlier. But if it is accompanied by the belief that the true effecter is only Allah, the Mighty and Majestic, then there is no harm in it, because this expression is in accordance with the outward order upon which the universe is established, namely the law of created causality. And there is no harm in seeking intercession through the messengers and prophets a fortiori: since it is the case that regarding medicine, the doctor, and rain there is no harm and no restriction, then it is even more fitting that there be no harm in that regarding the prophets, such as seeking intercession and seeking blessings through them. Indeed, Allah, the Mighty and Majestic, made His Prophet Muhammad a mercy to the worlds and clarified that in His clear Book when He said: {And We have not sent you except as a mercy to the worlds} [Al-Anbiya 107]. So He made him a cause for the mercy of the servants, and what harm is there in a Muslim seeking intercession through this one whom Allah honored with this honor and made a mercy for creation? And the Companions were all filled with awareness of this meaning, and they spared no effort in seeking intercession and seeking blessings through his relics and remnants on many different occasions, and this has been established by authentic hadiths that are not subject to conjecture or illusion.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage is a question-and-answer ruling on the permissibility of using causal language and seeking intercession (tawassul) and blessings (tabarruk). The author anticipates a question: what is the ruling for Muslims who use expressions like 'the medicine benefited me' or 'the doctor cured me', and similarly seek intercession through prophets or touch their relics? The answer distinguishes between two states: if such speech is accompanied by a belief that the thing itself (medicine, doctor, prophet) has an independent effect, then it is kufr by consensus. But if the speaker believes that the true effecter (al-mu'aththir) is only Allah, and these are merely apparent causes placed by Allah (the law of created causality, al-sababiyya al-ja'liyya), then there is no harm. The author then argues that if this is allowed for ordinary causes like medicine, then it is even more appropriate (awla wa ajdar) for the prophets, because Allah explicitly made the Prophet Muhammad a cause of mercy (rahma) for the worlds. The Companions themselves practiced tawassul and tabarruk with the Prophet's relics and traces, and this is established by authentic hadiths.

Correction: The author shows that such language is permissible when one understands that the true agent is Allah and that things are merely created causes (sababiyya ja'liyya).

Key Takeaway
Causal language and tawassul are permissible when one's belief is that Allah alone is the true effecter, and created things are merely causes.

?
If you caught yourself saying 'this medicine saved me' without mentally adding 'by Allah's will', would you stop and correct yourself? What daily habit could you adopt to keep the truth of al-mu'aththir (the true effecter) alive in your speech?

Refutation of Denying Tawassul After the Prophet's Death
The author refutes the argument that tawassul is only valid while the Prophet was alive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical inconsistency in rejecting tawassul after the Prophet's death while accepting causal language for worldly means, and the author's argument that this rejection is based on a materialistic misunderstanding of causality.

Core Idea
Denying tawassul after death is inconsistent with everyday causal language and implies a false materialistic view of the Prophet's role.

In this passage, the author exposes a striking double standard: the same people who freely attribute healing to medicine and doctors recoil in horror at tawassul through the Prophet, even though neither case implies independent causality. He then tackles the specific objection that tawassul was only valid when the Prophet was alive, showing that this objection misunderstands the nature of the Prophet's causal role, which is not tied to his physical life but to his divinely appointed status. This lesson unpacks this powerful refutation and clarifies the Islamic understanding of causality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the tone of astonishment the author uses. Identify the three main moves: the inconsistency charge, the causality clarification, and the refutation of the 'alive only' argument.
2
Analyze the inconsistency
The author points out that people who say 'medicine healed me' do not consider it shirk, but attack tawassul. Ask yourself: why is this inconsistent? What is the difference?
3
Understand the causality doctrine
The author states there is no real causality (sababiyya haqiqiyya) anywhere; all causality is created by Allah (sababiyya ja'liyya). The Prophet's created causality for mercy is far greater than medicine's for healing. Reflect on how this resolves the apparent contradiction.
4
Refute the 'alive only' objection
The author argues that limiting tawassul to the Prophet's lifetime implies his effectiveness depended on his physical life. This is a materialistic view and constitutes kufr. How does this refutation protect the integrity of tawassul?
5
Summarize the lesson
Write a one-sentence summary of the author's main argument against the deniers of tawassul after death.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والعجب من أناس يظلون ينسبون الأمور إلى أسبابها الشكلية بل كثيراً ما يعبرون بالألفاظ الدالة على أنها هي المؤثرة، كنسبة الشفاء إلى الدواء أو طلبه من الطبيب، ثم تراهم يُصغون بآذانهم إلى الناس حتى إذا ما سمعوا كلمة توسل أو تبرك بالنبي عليه الصلاة والسلام من أحدهم، انقضوا عليه بحكم الشرك والكفر، مع أنه لم ينسب إليه تأثيراً ولم يقل في حق رسوله أكثر مما يقولون هم في حق أطبائهم وعقاقيرهم. أفتكون سببية الدواء للشفاء أكثر من سببية سيدنا محمد للبركة والرحمة؟! أما نحن فنقول: لا سببية حقيقية هنا ولا هناك. ولكن هيهات أن ترقى هذه السببية الجعلية في الدواء والطب إلى معشار الدرجة التي ترقى إليها السببية الجعلية في محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم للرحمة والمغفرة والبركة. ومن العجب الذي يحار له العقل أن تراهم يقولون: إن الصحابة إنما توسلوا لأنه كان حياً بعد.. أما وقد توفي بعد ذلك فقد بطل التوسل به ولغا حكمه وعاد شركاً وكفراً!!.. فهل يقول هذا إلا من يحسب أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم كان يتخذ من حياته وقوته الجسدية وسيلة تأثير في المتوسلين والمتبركين به. فلما توفي ذهبت وسيلة التأثير فأصبح التوسل به توسلاً بما لا يملك أي تأثير؟!. وهل هذا إلا كفر محض، ووقوع في أسوأ مما يصطنعون الفرار منه.
And it is astonishing that some people constantly attribute things to their formal causes, and often express themselves with words that indicate that these are the effective agents, such as attributing healing to medicine or requesting it from the doctor, yet you see them lending their ears to people until when they hear a word of tawassul or tabarruk through the Prophet, peace be upon him, from someone, they pounce upon him with the judgment of shirk and kufr, although he has not attributed any effect to him and has not said about his Messenger more than what they themselves say about their doctors and drugs. Is the causality of medicine for healing greater than the causality of our master Muhammad for blessing and mercy? As for us, we say: there is no real causality here or there. But it is far from the causal causality of medicine and medicine to reach even a tenth of the degree that the causal causality in Muhammad, peace be upon him, attains for mercy, forgiveness, and blessing. And it is astonishing, bewildering the mind, that you see them saying: 'The Companions only sought intercession through him because he was still alive... but now that he has died, intercession through him is invalid and its ruling is nullified, and it has become shirk and kufr!' Does anyone say this except one who supposes that the Prophet, peace be upon him, used his life and physical strength as a means of effect on those who seek intercession and blessings through him? So when he died, the means of effect disappeared, and seeking intercession through him becomes seeking through one who possesses no effect?! And is this not pure kufr, falling into what is worse than what they pretend to flee from?
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of those who forbid tawassul and tabarruk through the Prophet after his death. The author points out the inconsistency: the same people freely use causal language like 'medicine healed me' but attack anyone who says 'I seek intercession through the Prophet' with accusations of shirk and kufr, even though the person does not attribute any independent effect to the Prophet. The author asks rhetorically whether the causality of medicine is greater than the causality of Muhammad for blessing. He then clarifies his own position: there is no real (haqiqiyya) causality in either case; all causality is created by Allah (sababiyya ja'liyya). However, the created causality of the Prophet for mercy and blessing is far greater than that of medicine. Then he addresses the specific objection that tawassul was only valid when the Prophet was alive. The author argues that this objection implies a materialistic understanding: that the Prophet's effectiveness depended on his physical life and strength. This is a form of kufr because it denies the ongoing spiritual role of the Prophet as a means of mercy. The author concludes that this objection is actually worse than the supposed shirk they are trying to avoid.

Man as Master of the Universe: A Sacred Role
Author concludes with man's mastery and intellect as sacred.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author balances Allah's sole causality with man's unique role as master of the universe, and the clarifying footnote that defines 'man' as the essence of humanity, not every individual.

Core Idea
Man is master of the universe, but only by Allah's delegation and within limits.

Having established Allah's sole causality through scientific proofs, the author now introduces a complementary truth: man's special station as master of the cosmos. This passage highlights how Allah's wisdom has subjugated all cosmic phenomena to serve humanity and granted humans a unique intellect. A crucial footnote clarifies what 'man' means here, preventing overgeneralization.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice that it begins as a summary and then introduces a new point about man's role.
Pay attention to the flow: from past proofs to a new assertion about man.
2
Identify the two key claims
The passage makes two main claims: (1) man is the master of the universe, and (2) man's intellect is a sacred reality enabling dominion. Find these in the text and note how they are justified by reference to Allah's wisdom.
The word 'sakhar' (subjugated) connects to the idea of divine causation.
3
Understand the footnote's clarification
Read the footnote carefully. It explains that by 'man' the author means the essence and reality of humanity, not every individual. Ask yourself why this distinction matters.
This prevents the claim from being false for individuals who don't exercise mastery.

فهذا مجمل ما يجب اعتقاده بالنسبة لشؤون الكون ومظاهره . وقد أوضحنا براهينه العلمية المتعلقة به . ولعلك قد علمت خلال ما ذكرناه ، أن حكمة الله عز وجل اقتضت أن يكون الإنسان هو سيد هذا الكون ، وأن تكون مظاهر الكون المختلفة من حوله مسخّرة لخدمته مسيَّرة لتحقيق مصالحه ، وأن يكون له من عقله - وهو حقيقة قدسية لم تمنح لغيره - ما يمكنه من بسط سلطانه على كثير من شؤون (۱) . This is the summary of what must be believed regarding the affairs of the universe and its phenomena. And we have explained its scientific proofs related to it. And perhaps you have learned through what we have mentioned that the wisdom of Allah Almighty required that man be the master of this universe, and that the various phenomena of the universe around him be subjugated to his service, directed to achieve his interests, and that he have from his intellect—which is a sacred reality not granted to others—what enables him to extend his dominion over many matters. (1) (1) We mean by 'man' his essence and reality, regardless of the enumeration of individuals. If we meant by it the species and all individuals, this statement would not be true, as is obvious.

Commentary
This passage serves as a concluding summary that also introduces a new theological point. The author states that the previous discussion has outlined the necessary beliefs about the universe. He then asserts that Allah's wisdom has decreed that man is the master (sayyid) of this universe, and that all cosmic phenomena are subjugated to serve his interests. Furthermore, man is uniquely endowed with intellect ('aql), which is described as a sacred reality (ḥaqīqa qudsiyya) not given to other creatures, enabling him to extend his dominion. A footnote clarifies that by 'man' the author means the essence and reality of humanity, not every individual human being; otherwise the statement would not hold true. This passage affirms the special status of humans in creation while maintaining that all causality ultimately belongs to Allah, as established earlier.

God as the True Cause and Tawassul as Worship
Summarizes causality principle and distinguishes tawassul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God alone is the true Cause of all causes, and how tawassul through the Prophet is distinguished from other means by being an act of worship.

Core Idea
All causes are formal; only God is the true Causer. Tawassul is worship, not just a means.

This passage from Shaykh Ramadan al-Buti summarizes a crucial principle: while the universe appears to run on causes, the only real Cause is Allah. Yet, using causal language is fine as long as we believe that. Then he applies this to tawassul—seeking intercession through the Prophet—showing it is not merely a causal link but also an act of devotion.

Guided Path
1
Understand the principle of causality
Read the passage and note that God is the sole Causer of all causes; all other causes are merely formal with no intrinsic effect.
2
Recognize permissible causal language
Notice that the author permits using expressions that align with the formal system of cause and effect, as long as one's certainty remains that God alone is the true Causer.
This prevents misunderstanding about everyday language.
3
Distinguish tawassul from other means
See that tawassul through the Prophet is included under this general principle, but it is specifically an act of worship (qurbah) because it emulates the Companions and glorifies the Prophet.
This shows tawassul is not just a causal link but a devotional act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخلاصة القول أن على المسلم أن يعلم بأن مسبب الأسباب كلها إنما هو الله عز وجل، فهي جميعها أسباب صورية لا قيمة ذاتية لها. فإن استعمل بعد ذلك في شيء من تعبيراته ما يتفق مع هذا النظام الشكلي للكون من قيامه على أسباب العلية والسببية، مع يقينه بأن الله هو وحده المسبب، فلا ضير في ذلك. ويدخل التوسل بالنبي صلى الله عليه وسلم في هذا العموم، ولكنه يمتاز عن التسبب بالمظاهر الكونية الأخرى، بأنه قربة من الله أيضاً، إذ هو تأسٍ بعمل الصحابة من جانب، وهو تعظيم لرسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم من جانب آخر.
And the conclusion of the matter is that the Muslim must know that the Causer of all causes is only God Almighty; they are all formal causes with no intrinsic value. If, after that, he uses in some of his expressions something that agrees with this formal system of the universe—based on causes and effect—while being certain that God alone is the Causer, then there is no harm in that. And seeking intercession (tawassul) through the Prophet (peace be upon him) enters under this generality, but it is distinguished from the other means of causation in the cosmos by being an act of worship (qurbah) to God as well, since it is emulation of the Companions' practice on one hand, and glorification of the Messenger of God (peace be upon him) on the other.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage presents the core principle of causality: God alone is the true Causer (musabbib al-asbab). All apparent causes are merely formal (sura) with no intrinsic independent effect. However, using everyday causal language—like when we say 'medicine healed him'—is permissible as long as the Muslim is certain that God is the sole Cause. The author then applies this principle to tawassul (seeking intercession) through the Prophet. Tawassul falls under the same category of acknowledging God as the true cause, but it is distinguished from other causal means because it is also an act of worship (qurbah) in itself: it emulates the Companions and glorifies the Prophet. Thus, tawassul is not merely a causal link but also a devotional act.

Key Terms
مسبب الأسباب
أسباب صورية
قربة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Using causal language (e.g., 'medicine cured me') implies believing in independent causes.
Correction: The passage clarifies that such language is permissible as long as one's certainty remains that God alone is the true Causer; the causes are merely formal.

Checkpoint
What distinguishes tawassul through the Prophet from other causal means in the cosmos?
A) It is the most effective means.
B) It is also an act of worship (qurbah).
C) It is forbidden.
D) It requires a mediator.

Key Takeaway
God alone is the true Cause of all; tawassul through the Prophet is a worshipful act, distinct from other means.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'this medicine healed me' or 'my hard work paid off,' do you truly feel in your heart that God alone is the Causer, or does a subtle sense of independent power creep in? What would it take to train your heart to that certainty?
?
In your moments of need, do you turn to the Prophet (peace be upon him) in tawassul as a mere means of getting what you want, or as an act of worship that glorifies him and follows the Sahaba? How can you transform that practice into genuine devotion?
?
If you were to be absolutely honest, do you sometimes treat the Prophet’s intercession as a 'spiritual tool' rather than an opportunity for worship? What would change in your supplication if you remembered that it is first a qurbah to Allah?
